On treatments for sores.
I have already said in the division of treatments for sores what is appropriate for a free person, and I say the treatment of those with more and with medicines according to their completeness.
Therefore, I say that sores are differentiated according to what causes them and according to the place on which they fall.
And the various causes by which sores are often formed are such as falling stones, or incisions of a sword or dagger, or striking with a spear, or with a staff, or with an arrow, or similar to these.
But the sores that are in the parts of the body are such as a sore that falls on the head, neck, foot, stomach, or buttocks, and similar to those of the limbs.
And I recommend the treatment for some sores based on this reasoning and rule for other sores.
And I begin with the simple sores of the head, as the complex ones will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, I say that when a simple sore occurs in the head and it is not a fracture, consider: if it was caused by falling stones or something similar, and the wound has already been stitched with the chain but is large and you fear the patient will develop an abscess, then immediately lance the cephalic vein and do not delay; and remove the pus from it according to the strength of the blood if there is no other reason prohibiting lancing, especially if the blood that has come out of the sore is little.
And the lancing should be on the opposite side of the affected part; and ensure that the patient does not eat or drink, or thick food.
But if there is any reason for the lancing to be prohibited, then it should be reduced by food and do not approach meat or wine.
Stop the sore if an abscess develops on it, and apply a hot oil or wine in which is steeped an herb that stops inflammation to it.
But if you are sure of the abscess, stop the sore, if it is fresh with its blood and air has not yet changed the powder that I recommend should be applied carefully.
And if the air has already changed it, stop the wound with some soothing agents until it is healed.
But if the sore is large or from an incision of a sword or similar, and the lips are not properly closed, then close it with suture as I recommend for their closure.
But if the sore is open to the bone and there is only a small suspension and no retention of it, then make an incision on it and treat the sore with medicines and restore the flesh in place of the skin, and instruct the patient to eat foods that are coarse: meats and broths similar to them, until it is healed.
And if the skin turns rotten and does not adhere to the bone, then make an incision all around it and cut everything out, and treat that.
But if in these sores there is an artery or vein from which blood can flow out and has not been separated by medicines, then ask about the artery, and if it is not separated, make a puncture on it with a spatula or lance it; and if necessity forces you to do what I have said does not conform, then cauterize it until the blood is separated.
And if these sores are simple and small, the cause is minor; therefore, sprinkle this powder on them as it hardens before the air changes it.
The method of the powder is as follows: mix one part of olive oil, two parts of dragon's blood (dragon's blood sap), three parts of non-extinguished charcoal; all must be well ground and sieved, fill the wound with it, tightly bind it with good binding until it adheres, and let it coagulate with the blood.
Then tightly bind it above with the powder and release it, but do not loosen it until it is healed, for the medicine has not been absorbed into it until it is healed.
Sometimes just make cautery (burn), when you do not have olive oil and dragon's blood sap at hand.
This method of treatment is done for large wounds that require binding and tightening when necessary, and for new wounds with their own blood, where the air has not yet affected them.
If the air has altered it, and especially in summertime, then do not intend to place this powder, but rather treat them with what was mentioned before, so that you bind them with appropriate dressings or that you bind them with oat flour paste mixed with water and honey until they become granulated; and apply the other treatment to them until they are healed.
But if there is a bone fracture in the wound, and it is small, then you extract that with forceps.
And you will say the treatment for skull fractures that are poisonous.

Of abdominal wounds.
The condition that occurs in the stomach is either great, small or moderate.
A small rupture is difficult to repair by other means, and it is known that the reduction of them is more difficult if you do not take care to push them back into their place as soon as possible, for it swells up and becomes enlarged, making re-insertion difficult.
Therefore, the best hernia is a moderate one, as in that case, the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, unlike the two types mentioned previously.
Know that the stitching of the stomach is according to four methods, but two of these are common and necessary in the stitching of other wounds on the body.
The other two methods are specific to other types of wounds, but they are particularly applicable in the stitching of the stomach.
I recommend that you expose and declare [the procedure] to everyone.
Thus say that if the wound is small and there is some cause of the hernia by it, and it is difficult to reduce it, then this can be done in one of two ways: either because of the small rupture as mentioned before, or when the intestine has become inflamed due to the coldness of the air.
And therefore, when this is the case, it is necessary that you heat the affected area by submerging a sponge or damp cloth in hot water alone, or by soaking it in warm water, and adding cyperus, spike, and bindings to push the inflamed intestine back into place until the inflammation is resolved.
In some cases, this can be seen where there is stiffness, and it is better to use water alone in the resolution of inflammation.
Therefore, when you see inflammation being resolved, then you moisten the intestine with water in which althea and mallow are mixed, as this helps to relieve the pain caused by it.
But if the intestine has not been pushed back into place with this treatment, then you make a small incision in the rupture using a sharp instrument to drain pus-filled abscesses.
The first sentence in English is: "The initial cure is better when it is not a section, and there should be no attempt to section unless strictly necessary and unavoidable."
"Of the instrument, choose its shape according to [the condition]:"
"One part should be angular and sharp, the other not-sharp, and the end should be subtle but not too subtle like a spatula, but rather slightly rounded."
"And since the abscess is enlarged and out of its place (hernia), it is necessary that its reduction should be in its natural form, in its proper place, if possible directly over it, because this is best."
"But when the rupture is wide and in the lower part of the abdomen, you must make the patient lie on their back, with their legs bent as much as their head; and if it is in a higher part of the abdomen, then you should raise their head and bend their legs downwards."
"And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the abdomen, you should always focus your intention and consideration on which side the abscess is closer to."
"And similarly, it is necessary that you treat both large abscesses and smaller ones."
"In small abscesses, place what you have prepared there, and make sure the one who administers it is among your most trusted, who will hold the rupture with his hand and adjust the edges of it, and carefully expose the abscess that may cause any obstruction to the suture."
"And this is the common method of suture: take a needle or counting needle according to the size of the abscess; and hold the needle from the edge of the rupture of the little finger, and tighten the needle one, without putting too much force in both ends of the thread, with both ends of the knot outside the edges of the incision until you make it penetrate from that part; and you have already added both ends of the thread and both ends of the knot, making four rows."
"And tighten with double force around the needle, and some watchful looks on both sides, and carefully bring together the edges and the abscess by stitching; and release the pressure of the finger and insert another needle in that place, and tie it in the same way as the first needle; and do not stop doing this until you have completed the entire contraction of the abscess with the needles."
"And the needles should be of a medium thickness between coarseness and fineness, because fine needles easily penetrate the flesh; and the large ones are difficult to insert into the knot, and therefore it is necessary that they should be of a medium thickness."
"And it is not necessary that the needle should be inserted at both ends of the incision immediately, because this may cause the flesh to be easily penetrated and the abscess to break before its consolidation."
"And do not prolong the stitching so that the abscess is prevented from consolidating."
"And carefully penetrate the ends of the needles so as not to cause pain to the patient while they sleep, and pause over the suture with expanded pulp of all parts, which hold the ends of the needles; and leave them there until you know that the abscess has consolidated."
"And these types of stitching with needles in this way are more suitable for small abscesses, because they require only one needle or two or around them."
"But the second stitching is common: and it is that you administer with the suture the four ends, of the incision and two ends of the knot, in one look, with a needle that should be tightly wrapped around its entire length."
"And when you have penetrated these four ends with the needle, withdraw the needle from that part where you started, to draw back all the pus from the upper part of the abscess towards the lower part, so that the stitching may be like sewing fabric where the fabrics are tightened."
"And pause between each stitch the thickness of a little finger."
"This stitching is known by men."
"And with this stitching I healed a wound that had occurred in a man's abdomen, which was covered with a coat of mail and was the rupture of a large abscess measuring one palm and protruding from two palms from the hernia of the lower part and the rupture was in the middle of the limb; and then I reduced it after the hernia had been out for 24 hours."
"And the abscess consolidated in the space of 15 days or more, and I cured him and he recovered; and he lived for many years and continued in his normal activities."
"And other doctors had judged that he would not recover; but by a miracle, because I did not cure him with the mouth (medication), because there was no place for it in his condition or any medicine, but instead I pressed on the abscess with a piece of old cloth two times a day and washed it constantly with honey water until he recovered."
Two kinds of stitching are said to be proper, according to the narrative of the Word.
And it is that you begin with sewing from the outside to the inside with the needle passing from the outer part to the inner part.
And when the needle enters the suture and passes through it and all along the body, leaving the tip of the thread at the point where you inserted the needle; then the needle is passed from the inner part to the outer part in the other end of the suture on the other side of the wound.
And when that has been passed through, you pass it again through the same opening from the outer to the inner side of the wound; and leave the tip of the thread at the point where this occurred, and pass the needle from the inner part to the outer part on the other side of the suture.
And pass through it with your penetration, which you passed through the wound at the point that you saw in the inner part of the incision where everything will pass through.
And begin with this passage on the side that is toward the point where you saw the suture, and pull out the needle from the suture that penetrated, and return it to the suture and bring the end of the suture to the opposite side of the wound, and pull out the other part of the suture from the suture.
And do this again and again, and continue to sew all that is outside following the same example.
And the entire method of this stitching is that you sew with skin sutures, which sews the skin, as you sew the wound once from the tip of the suture, and leave the other side until it is completed.
However, the second kind of proper stitching, which is called Gallic, and this is known from it and recommended for use, is that you sew according to a semilunar shape as some physicians sew in such a way, joining all the parts to their corresponding part by nature; and they apply the end of one to the other end and the point of the wound to the other end, and this is done as I have recommended.
You must insert the needle at the point of the wound on your side of the incision, and make it pass through to the inner part only, leaving two ends of the suture; then turn the needle and make it pass from outside to inside in two ends of the suture.
And turn each one, and make each one pass from the inner part to the outer part in two opposite ends of the wound.
And this method of stitching is better than common light stitching, and it is the stitching that I previously mentioned.
This is the Gallic method.
However, some say that the abdominal stitching is corrected with two needles, so you insert each one a thread and start with the entry of the needle on your side, and make it pass through to the other side as those who sew the heart do.
And you should know that when the rupture is in the middle of the abdomen, the stitching of it is more difficult than in other parts of the abdomen.

Treatment for fistulas and abscesses.
Know that every wound and abscess when it becomes old and decayed, and turns into an ulcer, and is not consolidated and exudes pus continuously without being drained, in any part of the body, universally it is called a fistula, and we call it an abscess.
A fistula in truth is a hardened node, white and painless with a cavity similar to that of an ulcer, and it is called a swollen abscess or "rizzo".
And in some men, it may be moist, exuding pus; and the moisture is usually drained in some hours. Sometimes this moisture becomes thicker and coarser, and sometimes it is more delicate.
I have shown all those causes, the signs of them and the treatment with medicine in the division.
And it is necessary that I inform you about the treatment for fistulas, which is performed by surgery with a knife.
And know that fistulas can occur in all parts of the body; those being fistulas that reach veins, arteries, nerves, spinal cord, hernia, bladder, ribs, spine, or joints composed by complexion such as hand and foot; and these are the ones that cause proximity to the main part and similar things.
And thus, the fistula of this kind, following this method, belongs to difficult-to-heal illnesses that do not accept cure and occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
If the depth of the fistula does not reach these places as mentioned and you hope for its healing, and wish to find out about it, then take a probe or iron, and if the fistula follows the correct path, look for it with it.
If the fistula is twisted, then investigate it with a probe of lead, as lead has the ability to be lightened and curved along the twists.
If the fistula is covered in horrible movement, it is not possible for you to investigate it with a probe.
Therefore, close the horrible openings by using a probe on them, as the moisture used to close that one will go to other openings and run out of them.
Then examine it with great care, to determine if you have bones or nerves, and whether the fistula is deep or shallow, or whether it is a fistula that has many horrible openings and is situated entirely on the surface of the patient, and the discovery of pain must be due to your compression on the area, and similar to the signs and causes of an abscess.
And when you have examined all of these causes with certainty, then go to healing with confidence: it is that you consider whether the fistula is superficial, near salt, distant from a joint, or near nerves, arteries, veins, or one of the places which I told you.
You then dry out the fistula as previously mentioned in the description of it, and remove whatever is in that drainage and rotten flesh, and whatever has been born in the sores of the skin or similar, and heal it within until it is healed.
If the fistula is deep and follows the correct path, then you must incise it as far down as possible for you, and clean out all of its rotten flesh; and apply hot poultices wrapped in medicines, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula, which you have filled with iron.
And do this several times until the hot poultice has destroyed all of the rotten material in the cavity of the fistula due to its corruption.
And then restore it with healthy tissue until it is healed.

On the care of sinuses and the quality of cleansing with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in any fleshy member and the space of the abscess is prolonged before collection of pus, it should be broken and perforated, and the pus is drained from there, leaving a hollow cavity resembling a vessel, and the dressing that is placed over it should be thin and not lead to further corruption, nor press on bones or nerves or ligaments. This is called a sinus, and it is not referred to as a fistula.
But if the space is prolonged before corruption sets in any member, it is called a fistula and not used.
And the treatment of this is to incise the entire dressing in the same manner as it is rolled up, especially when it is made thin and treated like cloth, and make sure it does not adhere to the area due to corruption.
But if you hope that the dressing will adhere and that corruption has not been achieved through subsequent consequences, and if the dressing contains pus on its surface, then you treat it with cleansing.
And if it is considered to be a sinus and it is large, and the stench from the arm is strong, then you should cleanse it with an Egyptian remedy that consists of oil, vinegar, and zenna root powder, which are mixed together in a vessel and cooked until they become a pure liquid.
Then you take the necessary amount from it (the Egyptian remedy), dissolve it in water and honey; then cleanse the sinus with this liquid, and tighten its opening, and leave the medicine there for a period of two hours.
Then remove this with care, and continue doing so for several days until the sinus is purified and the smell is gone.
And sometimes it is cleansed with water of ashes when this medicine is not available; and in such a case, you should take the ashes of vinegar and put them in water, then boil that solution and use it to cleanse the sinus until you are confident that the sinus has been drained.
If the patient does not tolerate the Egyptian remedy or water of ashes, then cleanse it with water and honey mixed with zenna root powder, or with vinegar and honey, because honey has properties to purify and clean, while vinegar makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is excess stiffness and dryness in the vinegar.
And when you have done this several times and are confident that the sinus has been drained, then cleanse it with a solution that makes the flesh grow there.
And you should take defyfinicon oil and dissolve it in rose oil and vinegar, or cleanse it with various remedies and ointments that have properties already mentioned in the treatise on ointments.
If the orifices of the sinus are narrow and cannot accommodate the canula of the clyster, you should gradually widen it with a small iron, pausing at each point where it is wrapped in Egyptian wool or Basilicon gauze until it is fully widened.
If the orifices are wide, you should add the lips to it with stitching and leave the quantity within the clyster unchanged.
If the orifices of the elbow joint are raised above the parting lines, then you should dry it in the affected area so that the arm can run into it below, because when the arm is bent at the angle of the sinus, flesh will be generated there.
If it is not possible to dry the sinus into the bone as you desire, then pierce the member to prepare it for the procedure by which the arm will pass easily through it according to how it suits you and that there is no obstacle preventing the arm from passing.

And it is not necessary when you want to release the arm and expel blood daily as the orifices of the vein are closed and it is difficult for blood to flow out, that you press hard on him with force and straighten it slightly, for this is very bad.

Of ventouse and of the qualities (i.e., uses) of those.

Title (Capitol) First
Summary and Compilation of the Causes of Fractures of Bones, from which the preliminary is necessary before we begin to recall the fractures of members and the various kinds of each (of them). Therefore, let us begin the introduction of this chapter by giving the summation of the discourse; and the chapters following will be understood by those who are experts and stationed at the truth of these subjects; so it is for you and for those who can learn or understand this noble art from you.
And therefore say that when a fracture or dislocation or twist or displacement occurs, it is necessary to begin with trepanation or ligation of the place, or whichever one, unless there is some prohibiting cause, such as debility in strength, or if it is the case that the person is at rest, or old age, or at a time of intense heat or intense cold.
And let it be filled with food consisting of lettuces and meats of birds and crabs; and forbid heavy meats and filling with food or any food that fills the veins with blood, until you are assured of a cauterized aposteme, and if there is no fear of effusion of matter in the member.
And then return the patient to their usual care with which they are accustomed.
And since the broken bone begins to be restored, it is necessary that the patient be fed with nutritious foods of thick and heavy consistency, in which there is viscosity, such as foods made from cooked wheat flour and water, rice, cabbage, peas, beef, veal, mutton, pork, large fish and similar ones, and with this regimen, coagulation of the fracture occurs more quickly.
And know that the bones that are broken in men of old age and the elderly cannot be continued and consolidated according to their nature due to the drying out of their limbs.
But nature adjusts and connects some cause similar to a glue on the broken bone in any part, causing swelling that adheres and strengthens it until it is also consolidated, until the end of softness and weakness comes, such as fire at the beginning, unless you prohibit this from its work.
And for this reason, it is necessary to stop the food of the patient, foods that are heavy, viscous and thick, as we have said.
And know that the types of fracture are diverse according to the diversity of the members, for a fracture of the bone in the thigh is different from a fracture of the bone in the head; and the fracture of the bone in the foot is different from the fracture of the bone in the hand; and similarly all other members are different from each other.
And I will come with a reminder of every type of fracture, explained in its own chapter separate from the others.
The diversity of fracture within itself is discussed below. The types of bone fractures are diversified in their details; for there is a curved fracture and one that occurs at the star, and some are fractured according to the length of the bone: and there are the fractures of the shin and ankle and addictions, such as those produced by blows and those not produced by them, such as the fracture in the penis when it is prepared for writing, and the fracture with a wound and disruption in the head, and the fracture with a crack.
And each type has its own remedy or torture in restoring it, as will be remembered separately for each one.
Sign of bone fracture. For this is how bone fracture is recognized: it shows twisting of the limb, and the prominence and appreciation of the injury in sensation, and it is that which you compress with your hand.
And therefore when there is no obvious twisting in the place, or it is not felt during examination, you must press the bone with movement and if the patient does not feel great pain, then it is not a fracture here.
It is possible that there may be twisting or a small crack, in which case you must press on it with your hand and compress it all; pause over the medicine of which the remedy appropriate to the place will be remembered at this moment; and it should be held tightly by traction.
And know that if the place is curved and not cut in two or something else does not occur in the shin, as for example, the entire circumference of its comparison is affected, then it is necessary to begin from your time at the rectification of it, having first applied cauterized aposteme.
And if a cauterized aposteme has occurred, leave it for several days until the aposteme ceases; and bind it according to any method that you can do with gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration of the limb and the alignment of the fractured bone is easier than in a shinbone where this has occurred.
And strengthen it according to what will be remembered for its remedy.
If there are splinters in the bones, then it is necessary to extend this broken member from two sides, if it is the hand or foot; or with your hand if the member is small; or with cords, or with one cord and the hand.
Place your position such that the injured member is extended according to its natural figure, while the parts are being extended from the broken member, then proceed to make it return to its original position with tools and in whatever way you can manage at their locations.
Be careful that your actions do not harm or injure the patient, and proceed as your strength allows to apply one bone to another in the best possible ways.
It is necessary at that time to apply your hand skillfully to it (the bone), and if you find any other cause there, correct it and align it according to the amount of your force.
Apply forceful extension and compression, as foolish doctors do when they cause abscess or caution to occur through their actions, or as we have seen happen through the action of those foolish doctors.
The quality of the tightening of a broken member is the one I recommend, because you should know that broken members are diverse in their smallness and greatness and in the shape of those.
And for those that are small, like the arm and the elbow and similar to these, it is necessary that the wrapping be of thin fabric.
But for those that are large, like the thigh or leg, it is necessary that the wrapping be broad and hard, because the thickness connects the large and strengthens it all over with tightening, in order to prevent infection.
And begin, align your evacuation with convenience, place firmly suitable coverings on it (the fracture), three or four depending on the member's need; and strengthen your hand slightly with the linen.
And bring it up to the uppermost part of the location of the fracture, and strengthen a little more than your tightening with which you have tightened the location of the fracture. And bring the wrapping of the location of the fracture out slightly, and loosen the tightening little by little, until you grasp a large part of the location.
And bring another bandage and wrap it over the injured location with the wrapping, and bring the wrappings to the part below the fracture; and your operation should be in tightening the bandage and loosening it according to what I have said in the first wrapping above.
And bring padding of leather or fabric to straighten the curvature of the fracture, if there is any curvature in it; and if not, do not place anything on it for any reason.
And place another bandage on it, and bring padding over these well-made stitches immediately if there is no inflammation or abscess in the location, and then place it on the abscess site and stop the inflammation; and leave it for some days; and then you strengthen over the stitches.

And it is necessary that the threads be moderate in thickness and fineness, and be of flax; and if the threads are thick, as I have seen by the operation of some fools using ropes of twisted flax, it is a great error when the narrowness equals [the affected area].
However, very thin threads are not suitable, because you will not achieve the desired narrowness with them, and it is necessary that between each stitch there be the width of one finger.
And if the patient is afflicted by the tips of the stitches near the narrowness in those places, then place a stopper made of beeswax or felt there to prevent injury.

And you should know that it is not necessary to suture all broken members immediately; the reason being that when a member is large, it is not necessary to stitch it on the first day but rather after 5 or 7 days, depending on your confidence in the development of an abscess.
And this is the method of plasters that ancient physicians used to restore, which involved suturing fractures, dislocations, and twists.
Common plaster method for fracture restoration, suitable for complex cases and more commonly for women and children, since it is on what neither heat nor friction acts: you must take powder from the mill, and it is the medlar pit that you should grind in the walls of the mill.

Another method of immobilizing a fracture or twist is to use an ointment made from myrrh, aloe vera, frankincense, and elm, all equal parts by weight of one drachm; white clay, half a drachm each; and Armenian pitch in large quantities.

Another method of applying a strong plaster for resolution, when an abscess lasting long occurs on the body: take radish root and its seeds, ballast nut, clover, marjoram, violet flowers, camomile, of all parts ground and crushed; all are steeped in water of savory or sweet water or vinegar, all according to the quality of the member and the relaxation of the color of it.
Hippocrates does not say in his book that it should be placed on a broken member for its restoration, but rather a plaster made of wax and oil should be applied and no other cause; and he recommends it to be neither too thick nor too thin.
Moreover, in Gaul it is seen that they should be applied on a broken member for its restoration and the causes in which there is dryness with little heat, such as myrrh, incense and similar to them of those causes that we have recommended.
Method of plaster for wounds and pain: take poppy seeds, celery, human skin scraped or goose feathers, radish, salt, ground and sieved; make it into a plaster.
Moreover, the quantity that corresponds to the extent of the wound and it should be removed, consider whether there is pain or swelling or if the bone is broken from its place; then do not remove for many days. And if it happens that the place becomes painful or swollen or inflamed, then cut or remove it at that time and apply plaster to it.
Take cloth and sea sponge soaked in warm water, submerge it in warm water, and with that wash the affected area until the swelling and pain cease; then let the member rest for an hour; and apply wool soaked in vinegar, oil or rose oil over it for one night until you are sure of the abscess being cauterized, and that the inflammation of the member is reduced, and the pain of it is relieved.
Also, apply gentle and small pressure to that slight constriction and do not tighten it with your first constraint, and gently until it is healed.
And when the abscess, redness, pain, inflammation and all the symptoms have been relieved, and you need neither plaster nor bandage, then massage it as you did at the beginning of the treatment.
And if no cause of those mentioned occurs in the member, then do not remove it except after three or four or six days. And after certain intervals all of this is left for twenty days according to your observation through the removal of the member, as we have said, until the fracture adheres and the flesh coagulates on it.
Then adjust the bandage more than your first bandage in all places, and adjust the swelling of the patient's wound according to the memory of it.
Moreover, if you see the location of the fracture drying out and extending more than necessary, then know that nourishment should not reach it; therefore wrap it in warm water daily to prevent it from drying out; and let this be for three days; and loosen the bandage slightly, and by this operation allow the member to receive nourishment, and heal lightly.
Those who restore the fracture of the member with other methods when it is not restored at the beginning, as necessary, and restore it incorrectly according to their operation, and have great fear, and if they were righteous, they would have increased these in their books and warned against doing it.
I have not seen anyone do this, so it is more reasonable that this should not be done.
Capital: On Fractures when they occur in the head.
The species of fractures are multiple, and the shapes of them are various, and their causes are distinct, because some fracture is caused by striking with a weapon, which shatters all the bone into small pieces, which is called scalping; or the weapon cuts some part of the bone, but does not penetrate to the back of it; and this species of fracture is called absolute elevation.
And the wound of these two fractures can be large or small, and whether it is contracted or protruded; and the cause of it is striking with a hammer or crossbow on stone, and similar.
This fracture is penetrating and approaches the panicle, which is adjacent to the bone; or it is in the face of the bone, and the wound of these two fractures can be large or small.
And this fracture is narrowed in the bone, in its subtlest part, and for that reason this species of fracture is called capillary fracture.
This fracture is caused by striking with a stone or impact on a stone, which causes one layer of the bone to enter into the internal layers, and it occurs in concave places such as at the joints when they are struck and split open and enter into the internal layers.
And moreover, it is done in bodies made of moist bones, such as those of children.
To all these kinds of dislocations and fractures, they are afflictions like separated needles. The patient will return to its place (position).
We must recognize all these kinds of fractures by observation on them and by inquiry into their symptoms, as well as the rotten flesh that is over them.
A capillary type (of fracture) is recognized by what is revealed over the bone and it is cleaned, and limited in size with a probe, because the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of fractures, it is necessary to consider first the accidents of the patient, and afterwards examine if they indicate by obvious signs of fear, such as vomiting of bile, extension, loss of consciousness, abscesses of the throat, fainting, acute fever, swelling and redness of the affected areas, and similar symptoms in the major dispositions, for it is doubted whether it will heal.
If there are no unusual accidents, you hope for health, then you begin its treatment.
The cause is that if it has been afflicted at the beginning as it is afflicted and this is during the days of winter, then you must study the removal of the bone fragment before the twelfth day according to all disposition.
And if it is during the days of summer, you must remove the bone fragment before the seventh day, because what remains of the bone can become rotten with putrefaction, and the accidents that we have mentioned may occur.
And if the fracture has already reached the pannicol covering the brain, and it is divided into small pieces and adhered (stuck together), then you must incise the divided area, compressed and adherent, according to what I will advise you.
It is that you reason the patient's head afflicted, and expose (examine) over the bone according to custom and as possible for you according to the appearance of the wound, and it should be gentle with the patient.
If it occurs to you, you must detect the bone, flow of blood or abscess, then you oppose yourself to it with what is necessary, and it is that you apply warm compresses made of wool soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess subsides, and be sure of blood flow; and then begin the circumcision of the bone and its removal.
And that may be one of two methods of operation.
The first method is that you incise the bone with a straight incisor and this is its form:
Then apply the incisor tool that is longer than the second one.
You must have several incisor tools of various sizes, some longer than others; and some shorter than the rest and should be at the end of sharpness of their tips, and made of iron or steel.
Use them (the incisors) easily when striking on the instrument to avoid hitting the skull and damaging it.
If the bone is strong and hard, then you must perforate around it before administering the incisors with chisels (taravels), which are called non-penetrating chisels.
They are not called that because they do not pierce the end of the bone to where it is near, for this reason the chisel has a rounded tip or edge over what is left of the cap of it sharp, similar to an edge or small circle, which prohibits being submerged and passing through the bone.
It is appropriate for you to take a large number of these splinters that each one corresponds to the specific bone, until it is present in all parts of the skull, the end of which is sharp. The quantity in length and width should be according to the size of the bone splinter.
Here is the form of three types of splinters: The quality of perforating around the broken bone is that you place the splinter on the bone and twist it with your fingers until you know that the bone is pierced; then move the splinter to another location, and stop when it reaches a place where the splinter's size matches the length or thickness of the ray.
Here is the translation: Then insert a chisel and chisels between each crack of the bone, and make a subtle incision on it at the end of levitation, as we have said, until you cut the bone, or with your hand, or with some other means using various tools that you will have prepared for this purpose, such as mallets and forceps.
You must be very careful that the chisel does not touch any of the nerves.
Therefore, when you perforate the bone and it is continuous with the nerve if it is so, you must separate it from the nerve and extract it. You should cut and file it on the roughness of the bone with another tool similar to a chisel, but be sure that it is thinner and more delicate than other chisels.
And if any small pieces of the bones remain and are stuck, you must take it with ease with what is appropriate from the tools at your disposal; then care for the wound with linseed oil and ointments that we have mentioned.
Here is the translation for this line: The other method of operation is easy and far from fear, which we call Galen's dry method, and it is highly praised; this is its explanation: It is necessary for you at the beginning to begin by uncovering the fracture site where the break is most evident and visible. After you have discovered that fracture and have stopped at the place with the slow and gentle tip of the chisel, which has the following shape:
The round fracture is long and does not cut any nerve, while the sharp fracture has two edges advancing in length, as sometimes happens; that the round fracture should be adjusted to the nerve with the sharp edge cutting into the bone.
Then strike the chisel with a small hammer on one side of the fracture until you have cut through the entire bone with ease as it rotates, and you are sure that no panic occurs in any way in case of accident that worries you; if the patient was very foolish and most ignorant among all men, certainly he was asleep.
If any nerve remains attached to the bone of the panic at certain locations on the bone, you will cut it with the round tip of the chisel and release it gently without harm or fear.
If the bone fracture does not penetrate into the panic, and what is separated or remains of the bone is its face, and it remains rough and subtle indentations, you must file that roughness and remove those indentations with fine files which you have prepared in various quantities so that you can administer them in all places at the most convenient time and the right place for each one depending on where you find yourself in your operation of that broken bone and the shape of it.
You must use reason when filing the bone at first with the least of those files, and then use the most delicate part of it, without ceasing to do so continuously until you reach the point where you can administer the thin and fine parts of all of them.
But in the case of other small and subtle fractures, you must care for it according to what you find for its correction.
The reason is that it is not completely understood in all aspects by those who study that art. And we will write about what we have shown for large fractures.
If the panic is rectangular when you cut the bone, then you must take cloth in accordance with the size of the wound and immerse it in rose oil and place it over the orifice of the wound; and take another cloth doubled or tripled, immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the primary cloth, and apply gentle pressure on it that you can apply without damaging the nerve; and administer a thin ointment to it, but do not press too hard on it, only as much as the wound requires.
Leave that patient in this care for a day or two until you are certain of the abscess cauterization; then separate him and administer medicines suitable for dryness, such as linen rag and oat flour, powder of figs and Aristolochia, and similar ones.
It is that you make a powder of these medicines and sprinkle it over the sore, so it becomes dry; and in general, administer to him all medicine with properties that absorb and do not irritate.
Be careful that the sore in your care is clean; it should not be covered with grease or anything else; nor let it fester; for when it becomes infected in the brain tissue, it corrupts it and poisons the patient, and leads to a great evil.
When the skull is opened, as the bone is cleansed and especially when the treatment of it is prolonged, and it becomes black on the surface; therefore consider whether the patient has experienced the accidents that we have mentioned, know that it is dangerous without any doubt.
If the blackness does not come from the medicine applied, but is due to the properties of that medicine, then you must take a part of honey and three parts of rose oil. Take a linen cloth and lay it over the skull; place a poultice of appropriate healing herbs on top of it until it is healed.
Chapter III.
On the restoration of the nose when broken.
Know that the nose is not broken into two halves as it is sometimes mistakenly thought, but one of them is slightly displaced or observed, because in it there are two bones, and the break is not complete, but rather a dislocation, twisting and narrowing.
But if one of the two halves of it is broken, you must insert your little finger into its nose and guide the fracture inward with your other finger from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape. Make it as easy for you as possible, and be careful that your operation does not harm the patient.
If the fracture is in the superior half of the nose and the lower one does not project, then you must equalize it with the end of the bone that is slightly thickened.
If the fracture is in both halves, then you do the same.
Gather all the necessary materials for its repair on the first day of the fracture, if possible; and if not, wait until the seventh or tenth day for the abscess to heal.
Insert a piece of linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one half, or two calques (small pieces), if the fracture is in both halves, and make it as thick as it fills the hollow of the nose.
As the old restorers say, the calques should be sewn with thread and kept in place all day, but I don't find it so; nevertheless, the cloth must be folded in albumen of coagulated egg white with powdered flour and placed inside until the bone is healed and the cartilage hardened.
In some cases, it may be better to put something inside the nose instead of a calque, such as a piece of lion's skin, if there is excessive bleeding; and if the fracture of the nose is severe and the patient is not forbidden from breathing through it.
This procedure is not necessary for you; if you want, do it, and if you want, use calques.
If there is another abscess that needs healing, fill the nose with wax or a mixture of honey and rose oil or balm, or alum.
But if there is no abscess that needs healing, you must fill it from outside with oat flour and powdered figs mixed with egg white; and place a piece of lion's skin over it, but do not let the nose touch anything.
But if the bones of the nose are broken with a small fracture or comminuted, you must break it further and extract them with the appropriate instruments; then sew up the opening, heal it with what strengthens and consolidates, and use plasters that are suitable for healing.
If there is a sore inside the nose, you must heal it with calques and administer lead tubes until it is completely healed.
Chapter IV.
On the restoration of the jawbone when broken.
When the lower jawbone is broken and the fracture is not in a sore, observe whether the fracture is only on the outside, not split into two and curved towards the inside, then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on the right side, you must insert your left index finger into the patient's mouth; and similarly, if the fracture is on the left jawbone, insert your right index finger into their mouth; and push the curvature of the fracture towards the inside with ease at the inside parts, with your other hand pushing the outside part of the bone; do this gently.
And if the jawbone fracture is separated into two, then it's necessary to administer extension of both parts according to right angle until the alignment can be achieved.
If there is a fracture or dislocation of the teeth, take care to set those that can be saved with gold, silver, or ivory splints; then place cotton on the broken jawbone and also double layer of linen cloth, and a thick plaster well made or a single piece of gauze along the length of the jawbone; bind them as appropriate according to the fracture type and necessary application until it is not destroyed.
Order the patient to rest and take only mild food, avoid sour lemons.
If you suspect that the figure has been altered in some way, wait until the third day and then examine it; correct what is already altered with powder of milled pearl, albumen of egg white or flour of chalk, because you will remove the cotton from it, and place lamb wool on the plaster.
Then leave the plaster attached to it, and if the bone arrangement is not altered, let it be, until it heals and strengthens the fracture; because this fracture usually heals in three weeks.
If another pus-filled boil occurs, administer what was mentioned earlier in its treatment until it is healed and the boil is removed.
If the fracture is a wound, consider whether it is separated flesh or an ulcer; in this case, carefully separate those tissues with the appropriate tool for removing them with instruments.
If the wound of the ulcer is narrow, expand it with a trocar according to your need.
Then, when you have removed those tissues and they are not reabsorbed, close the wound if it is large; otherwise, cover it with one of the appropriate dressings and let it heal.
Chapter V.
On the restoration of the collarbone when it is broken.
In three ways, the collarbone can be broken at its juncture with muscles; and this fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Because it is broken, separated, or separated into two parts other than those that become ulcers; and it is easier to treat the former; or it occurs in such a way with ulcers, and it is difficult to treat the latter; or it is like when the fracture occurs with an open wound.
And the operation when the fracture occurs without an open wound is that you must have two assistants present; one to hold the broken collarbone and another to extend the neck in the affected area.
Then align the fracture with the teeth mentioned earlier until the proper shape is achieved without excess protrusion or concavity.
If more extension is needed, it's necessary to place a pillow or wool beneath the patient's back and adjust its size according to your need; then extend and elevate the collarbone and hold it in place with your hand until you align the fracture as necessary.
If you are unable to insert the extremity of the collarbone into its proper position outside, because it is inaccessible due to a previous incision, then the patient should be repositioned on their back, and a small cushion placed on the affected muscle.
The assistant should press the muscle at the fracture site until the collarbone is elevated so that it aligns with the incision above; then you must correct the fracture and align it with the teeth mentioned earlier.
But if there are ulcers on the collarbone and they are bad, it's necessary to make an incision over that area and remove those ulcers with ease.
If it is retained in the bone, you should make an incision into it with one of the instruments you have prepared for this purpose, after placing the chisel that serves as a lever below the collarbone; and the chisel has a shaft or iron in the shape of:
It is like the cupler (hollow part) of which this is the concavity; and let its width be as required for it, according to what you have needed for it, according to the size of the bone or the scarcity of it.
The length should be as far as is possible for the operation; it must have two ends in this way, and one end should be wide and the other narrow.
If the tear of the wound that you have incised when you extracted the pus from the bone is wide and it appears to be an abscess, then you adjust the two lips of the wound with stitches.
If the tear is small or you fear abscess, then you fill the wound with cloth and powder according to the size of the wound's incision.
If abscess occurs, then you insert linen in strong vinegar and rose oil and wait on it.
And in all, the splint should not be harmful to the broken bone; and for this reason you must see the patient every day, because when the limb is softened or the splint has been removed, then you correct it and strengthen the fracture.
You place the patient's head on their neck and let them sleep slightly inclined from that side; so that with this, the support of the limb from that side is elevated, and thus the fracture of the vertebra is elevated to the elevation of the muscle; or the arms are attached to the neck of it.
And do not loosen the limb unless abscess occurs in the location or there is pressure, within twelve days.
And you renew the plaster if you have seen its face and adjust it in the stricture and restore it, and leave it until it is restored and the fracture of the vertebra is coagulated.
And it should be strengthened and supported for twenty-eight days, and sometimes in some men it is done in the month of that time.
Chapter VI.
On the restoration of shoulder fracture.
The spatula is broken at the lateral location, but not completely broken, only the edges.
Therefore, when the location of it is broken - and it is broken in the middle location - and it is not known except by touching, then you prepare it according to the shape of the fracture and reduce it according to its natural figure in all ways possible with any means at your disposal.
And you place powder of flour with egg white and leopard skin, and you place double powder of cloth, and you place a thin table-steel rod on it according to the size of the spatula or more if necessary.
But if the rod is concave in some of the locations of the spatula, then you fill that concavity with leopard skin, and you adjust the rod according to equality.
And strengthen it from above with a long emetic with appropriate constriction and the firmness of the rod until it is removed from its location.
Consider the limb every day; and every time it softens, the next day you strengthen it equally and adjust the rod if it has been removed from its location.
Keep the patient on their side.
The spatula is restored in twenty or thirty days.
Therefore, when this number is completed, then you loosen the limb, and you know for sure that it is of the bones from which the fracture did not begin or their dissolution.
And if there is pressure in the bone location and you place a bandage, then you make an incision on it, and remove it, and do as previously described in the vertebra fracture to avoid abscess danger if it occurs here.
Chapter VII.
On the restoration of foot fractures.
The foot is sometimes broken in the middle location, but the ends of it move towards each other, but they are swollen and bruised.
And when the bone of him who is fractured breaks apart, it descends into parts inside and happens that he experiences great pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, fever, and vomiting blood; and it is revealed in the cavity of the broken bone and not hidden from sensation.
And the restoration is that the patient lies on their back, and a cushion is placed between their shoulders; press firmly on the muscles of him, and adjust his ribs with both hands, be subtle in the operation of him, and in the alignment according to all means possible and necessary, until the figure of the bone returns as it should.
And place a plaster and a stopper on it, and lay a thin strip of willow or caligy wood with similar properties on top of it, because you cover him with cloth; be subtle in tying that one over the fractured bone so that it is not too tight; and go along with the curvature of his back some times, and tighten it appropriately; consider the tie at all times, and when it becomes soft, tighten it again.
If necessity causes the plaster to come off, you must corrode or burn it, or pain, or an abscess that occurs in the location, then you cut and remove it and remove the plaster and correct what happens due to it with a method of treatment for him; and return the plaster, if necessary, to its place; and adjust its tie until it is healed.
Chapter VIII.
On the restoration of broken ribs.
Know that a fracture of the ribs does not happen in thin places that follow the spine; and from the ends of them on the front side it is not contracted except by what is cartilaginous. And the knowledge of this should not be hidden from sensation, the investigation of it should be done with the aforementioned signs.
And the restoration should be such that the fracture is closed with the aforementioned methods, until it is healed as necessary, and place plaster and tighten the broken bone with a splint if it is required.
And if the fracture of the ribs descends into parts inside, then the patient experiences intense pain and puncture, as in puncture that occurs in someone who has wept, and because the bones are covered with linen; and difficulty in breathing, coughing, vomiting blood; and this is difficult to cure.
And the ancients were skillful in that with many clever methods.
And that which they said is: it is necessary to stop the food of the patient that generates inflammation and gas, so that the abdomen is inflated and expanded, and the fracture is forced outward to the parts outside.
But we should beware of this, so that it does not cause the creation of an abscess cautiously, if not necessary; and if it happens, then help with this.
And some of them said that it should be stopped above the bladder and that it should be squeezed out forcefully; and this is reasoning, but it is feared that the bladder attracts excess to the location due to weakness.
And some of them said: it should be covered with linen that is already immersed in hot oil, and pulverized material between the ribs until it is filled, let the tie be tight when you cover it according to the curvature; and cure the patient with treatment for pleurisy and medicine.
And if intense constriction causes suffering for the patient that cannot tolerate it, and the bones are pierced with a linen cloth with puncture, beware of the patient, then it is necessary to make an incision over the location, uncover the broken rib, and place an instrument over it that preserves the cipher, the memory of which was mentioned earlier, and cut the bone with ease and extract it.
And place both lips of the wound together, if it is large, with sutures, and heal it with plasters until it is healed.
And if an abscess occurs inside, then you cut and pour hot water into it and place plasters over the location, and cure the patient with what is necessary for an internal abscess.
And lie on a board over which he sleeps until healed.
Chapter IX.
On the restoration of fractures of the spine and neck.
Sometimes fractures occur in the bones of the neck, and later they contract, similar to the vertebrae of the back. And if you want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider, because if his muscles are already softened and swollen and dead and cannot be made to grow or strengthen, and when you tighten them with the aforementioned ties or pierce them with a needle and do not feel pain there, then you know that it is not healed, and in the month of preparation it is mortal.
And if you feel constriction and pain with the ties and piercing in those, then you know that the neck of the bone is saved and that the patient will recover with treatment.
And if you see the vertebrae of the neck that resemble this and want to know whether it will heal or not, then consider the weight of it.
When you see that it has become softened, go to those who have occurred in the thighs, and approach when he lies on his back, there is ventosity and evacuation without his will, and sometimes he is reclined on his back and wishes to urinate but cannot, then you know it is fatal, and at that point do not work on the healing of him.
But if no such cause occurs in this case, the cause is better and easier to handle.
The treatment for him is to perform the cautery of the abscess that you place over the swollen vertebra or oiled with oil, or with honey applied; place it over that for several days until the abscess is cured.
Then place one of the comforting and existing plasters, and strengthen it there with liniment; and command the patient to rest and remain calm and not sleep on the side where pain occurs until it is healed.
And if it happens that the fracture of the bone occurs or any other cause that has already been separated from him, then you must cauterize the skin over the wound and open the bone; and align the two lips of the wound, with suturing if it is large; and care for it with a dressing made of grease until it heals.
If it is broken at the end of the bone in the heel, and that is the end of the tendon, then you must insert your left hand into the anus and grasp the fractured bone with the other hand as possible by equal measure; and place a dressing and splint over it if needed, and strengthen it.
If you feel fractures in it (the bone), then cut it open and remove it, and care for the wound with a treatment that is preceded until it heals.
Chapter X.
On the repair of the anus.
The bones of the anus are broken and if they break, the fracture is not only communicated to the extremities of these bones but they are inserted in length and decline into the sides; and pain occurs in that area and a lump forms when it is fractured.
And the repair of it (the anus) is that you extend your hand over it to the fracture which is the figure of it.
And if the fracture is only at its extremity, you grasp it as you are able to grasp it in accordance with the equality of grasping something that is similar to its natural shape.
But if the fracture is in the length or that declines into the sides of the anus, then you make the patient lie on his stomach until you are prepared to repair the fracture. Therefore when you have grasped it (the bone), place a dressing over it, and also place the splint of wood or skin on it, and strengthen it with suturing, without fear of the fracture being punctured or the removal of the splint.
Grasp the concavity with whatever you fill it until it is held by the suturing according to equality, and command the patient to sleep on his back or his right side.
But if an abscess occurs, you cease the extension and restoration of it until the abscess is cured, and place on it what you use to soothe it according to what has been done before; and return to its repair and strengthen it as necessary.
And if the bones are fractured or shattered, you treat them.
Chapter XI.
On the repair of the fracture of the adductor (muscle).
The adductor is that which is between the thigh to the head of the shoulder.
And if it is broken, the repair of it (the adductor muscle) is one of two ways, of which one is that you take a flat splint of medium thickness in this shape:
And let them be attached to two extremities with liniment, and let it be suspended from a raised location.

And support it with some serious cause, or else the minister extends it to the sides; and the doctor who has set the fracture should hold it with his hands until the fracture returns as it should.
Another method is that the patient is laid back on their neck and their hand is supported at the neck; and command two servants, one of them to hold what is above the fracture with his hands; and the other to hold the foot below it; and each extends to their side.
If you want the extension to be more forceful, then you press strongly on the fracture with both hands and on the other side with another hand, and each servant extends to their two sides.
Therefore, if the fracture is near the end of the muscle, it should be stopped halfway at the shin and the other side of the fracture must be tied.
Similarly, if the fracture is near the joint, it should be stopped at that location and also at the joint.
Wrap it gently with ease and without much effort, and when it is properly tied according to the situation and applied well, then press it if there is not an abscess formation.
But if there is an abscess cautery, leave the wrapping for up to seven days and press on it with a wool suture dipped in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess recedes, then press it.
The method of pressing the fracture is to apply a plaster on it and wrap a seaweed cloth around the plaster; and adjust the arm in a splint, and place your wet hand over the muscle.
And wrap the cloth and press on the splint to keep the arm in its place, unless there is any reason due to a broken bone.
And use of splints.

And press on the splints as previously mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And your wrapping over the location of the fracture should be tighter. And as the fracture becomes longer, the wrapping should be looser.
And if the situation of the splints and wrapping is as previously mentioned when you restore the limb to its proper position, then do it.
