And consider the ligament in all parts for three days to prevent pus or inflammation in that location, or if food is not allowed to reach the limb due to excessive wrapping; and therefore correct everything as previously mentioned.
But if you are confident that there will be no complication of this, do not remove the ligature until at least three days or more.
The patient's bed should be on their side and their hands on their stomach.
And wrap the cooked arm full of wool padding, and consider at all hours of the night or day that the figure of the broken limb should not be unbound or dissolved, or that the ligament is softened; therefore correct everything with your judgment.
And restrict the patient's food as previously ordered, so that it is initially subtle until it starts to thicken, and then it must be made coarser.
For the custom of the splint and leg rest is that they should be strong for forty days, and then they must be loosened and a bath given and medicine applied with enema, which will correct it.
And if the fracture is laid flat, do not loosen the ligament and splint for up to two months.
Chapter XII.
On the restoration of the fracture of the arm.
The arm consists of two bones that are called the two focils; the smaller one is the one we call the head, and the larger one is the one that is placed below the smaller one, from the lower part.
And it can be broken only the major focal or the minor one, and they are broken together.
And when the small focal is broken above, the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the large focal is broken below, the fracture of it is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And even worse is when two bones are broken together.
Therefore, if the bone that is broken is the small one above, it is appropriate for the doctor to, wait a slight extension with ease until it is wrapped.
If the large focal is the broken one, then it is necessary to wait for a more vigorous extension.
And if both focals are broken together, then you must wait for the setting with less force.
It is necessary to wait for the figure of the hand in order to restore it and extend it on a cushion; the wrist should be at the sides above, as all the others have said, and the thumb should be below the other fingers, and the patient should be seated on a low stool.
And let a servant be in front of the patient at his head or feet, so that the patient does not tire.
Then let another servant extend his arm from the opposite side and hold him, and let another minister do the same.
Then let the doctor insert his finger into the bone to reduce it as best he can.
If there is a fracture of the bone, then you must try to reduce it all by your virtue.
If there is a separate fracture that cannot be healed and you do not expect recovery, then cut it off as we have mentioned above.
But if the fracture is infected, you have already read about that in a separate chapter, and therefore take care of it.
If an abscess arises at the beginning of your treatment, you must line a cloth with a mixture of rose oil and white wax that is medium in thickness and strong on one side with fine suture thread to contain the abscess; then remove the wax and apply a prepared plaster made of powdered milled chalk and clay of huou, and place needles nearby; and let it be the needle which is held over the fracture tightly and firmly.
And know that the number of needles in the arm is usually in major arrangements, or if the fracture is in a socket or in two sockets together.
And place your suture over the location of the fracture as firmly and as strongly as possible, and when it is extracted at the parts above or below the fracture, loosen the suture slightly, according to the reminder given at the beginning of this chapter.
And the cloths that are wrapped around the fracture should be soft and moist, not hard or coarse; and the thread with which it is sewn should be of medium thickness, as we have recommended.
Consider the member and let it rest for several days. And if any cause arises for which rectification is necessary, such as swelling of the member, then you must bind the member with a cold water poultice until it stops.
Release the bandage and allow the member to rest, then tighten the suture.
If the swelling has been softened and the bone has healed and appears like those, then you must correct everything as best you can.
Consider more if the nourishment is prohibited from reaching the member due to excessive bandaging, for then you must loosen it slightly and allow it to run for several days until it reaches the nutrient, and strengthen it.
If the patient does not have any of the aforementioned causes, then it is not necessary to undo the bandage for more than about twenty days or so.
Then let the hand of the patient be suspended by its virtue of moving in an agitated manner, and have him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture is healed in thirty days or forty days, or a little faster in twenty-eight days, and all of this depends on the complexion and the virtues.
Chapter thirteen.
On the treatment of a fracture in the little finger or fingers.
Know that in the little finger and joints of the fingers there can be a fracture, but this does not usually happen unless it is injured by strong movements.
And when there is a fracture or injury in the hand, then you must have the patient hang down with his arms stretched out and place his hand on top of it.
Then let another servant extend the broken bones and the doctor insert them back into place with good alignment.
And you must place plaster and stop it if there is not an abscess, and place needles on top according to the size of the area, and you have already wrapped it in cloth.
But if the break is in the inner part of the palm, then you must make a sling-like bandage and command the patient to hold it with his hand in a closed position; let it be strong with long cloth.
And the needle of the heart should be made of cloth with light material, to ensure that it is applied to the palm; and tighten it as necessary.
But if the fracture is in the outer part, you must place needles on top and another needle at the opposite side of the palm, so that the hand is open and straight; and tighten the suture as needed. The hand should be turned to the ratchet between the fingers with padding.
And if there is a fracture in one of the joints of the fingers, then if it is the thumb, insert and tighten it as needed and hold it with the palm; if you wish, place a small straight needle under it to raise the fracture and prevent it from going down.
But if the fracture is in other fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, ring finger, or little finger, then insert and align it with the digit that is next to it; or all of them together, which is better; or place a small needle on top, as we have said for the thumb.
Consider in your treatment, arrange carefully the position of the cauterized abscess and oppose it – with its own operation – whatever occurs due to any cause of that, as is repeated in the narrative.
Chapter XIV
Treatment of fracture of the bone of the thigh.
The fractured bone of the thigh, when observed, is broken and is diagnosed by sensation according to whether it moves or is covered in pus.
And the treatment of it is done in such a way that a strong ligament is placed over the fracture and another ligament below the fracture, and the patient lies on their stomach and on their face.
And the minister extends all the ligament to its separation according to its quality, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
But if it is in the region of the thigh, then the ligament should be near the back at the separation of the adductor muscles, so that the extension fits above it, and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the break is near the back, then make the ligament near the buttock and extend it to the separation below.
And the doctor presses the bone with both his hands inside it as much as he can reduce it according to its natural shape and the bone is joined well by a good union; then it is necessary to apply plaster and bandage, unless the member has an abscess. But if an abscess occurs, then leave it for several days until the abscess is drained, and return to your treatment.
The bandage should be wrapped in this way: cover the fracture with a hard bandage, fold it two or three times, and remove any excess; then wrap around the leg to reach the ankle and the region of the thigh.
Insert a long splint between the thigh and the leg near the back at the separation below, and the ends of the splint should reach the area above the wound on both sides; then turn it around the leg and thigh so that any excess of the bandage is covered.
Place a cushion on the thigh at the location of the fracture splint, and place another wooden pin over the bone of the leg.
And fill the hollow between the leg and thigh with soft cloth, so that the hematoma is coagulated.
And begin with a strong bandage in the middle over the location of the fracture with three or four wraps, and as you approach the ligament near the fracture, your bandage should be less tight and more elastic and more moist.
Extend the splint to both ends below to reach the ankle of the foot, and tighten both the ends of the pins on the other side so that the bandage does not move from its location.
Approach the structure over that which is so that it does not affect the member with corrosion or abscess, cautery or inflammation and resembles those; and if any cause of these occurs, then you must dissect it from there, and correct everything that has happened appropriately, as many observed.
But if there is pain in the bone that grinds it down, then you must hold it as possible for you; and if not, make a splint over it and remove the affected part, and heal with what was described earlier until it is healed.
Some fractures of the thigh are strengthened by this which are adjusted to the splints, just as it was said in the adjunct and in the arm.
But in the restoration of it in such a manner does not happen to the patient with limping; and if it is restored alone, another part that is applied to the other leg, then it is necessary for the one from whom this always limps.
And know that the thigh is strengthened in forty days or a few months, or a few weeks, and all this is according to the diversity of complications and other arrangements.
Chapter 15.
On the restoration of the fracture of the heel bone.
Know that the fracture of the heel bone takes a long time to heal, but it happens many times with delayed healing.
But if there is a fracture, then it is not only fissure or crack in its parts, and this is with a wound or without wounds, and all this is with the sensation.
The restoration of it is such that the broken parts are held together with what was described until they are united and joined as much as is possible by alignment, ease, and fabrication.
And place a plaster and a pad over the rounded stem if needed; strengthen it with a splint appropriate for that purpose.
And consider all the arrangements that were recommended in other fractures, such as abscess and similar ones that oppose all accidents with what is appropriate for it until it is healed.
Chapter 16.
On the restoration of the fracture of the thigh bone.
Know that in the thigh bone there are two bones of which one is large, and it is called by the name of the thigh; and the other is small, and it is called the femur.
And if any of the types of fracture occurs in two bones of the arm, and it is done by restoring the arm as described, and the operation on it is one.
If the bones are fractured together, the thigh bone is broken in all parts.
And if the small bone is fractured, the thigh bone is converted into many pieces in front.
And if the large bone is fractured, break it below, and this is due to causes that are not known to you.
And provide extension, alignment, and ligature of the tendons according to the restoration described.
But if the fracture of the thigh bone is shallow and has few setbacks, then the extension should be less and lighter, and ease with your skill.
And in the thigh bone there is some cause of operation on the arm, and this is when you hold the splints and complete all the operations described, then you will take pieces of wood from pine which are placed under the causes that have been placed on a shelf, and these are made exactly as branches of palm; align them according to the small curvature, and they should not be too heavy for the hands, and the length of these according to the length of the thigh bone, and from the heel to the foot.
And wrap each one with fabric in two layers according to its length, and place one over the fracture of the thigh bone on one side, and the other over the fracture of the other side, from the heel to the sole of the foot.
And align the two splints in three locations at both ends, and in the middle, because with this structure the thigh bone is prohibited from bending on one side or the other, and it stands well.
And a brace made of wood according to the length of the thigh bone is administered; it should be placed under the thigh to prevent movement.
Furthermore, you must do this in a fracture, and when with that it is truly a wound.
And consider in each day the thigh bone and study it with more care, and keep it from abscess and inflammation or other similar causes; and when any cause of these occurs, then you must oppose it until it is healed.
Chapter 17.
On the fracture of the ankle and bones of the foot and toes.
Fracture does not occur in the whole ankle; but in the bones of the foot it happens occasionally; and in the toes of the foot, few occur fractures, and these do not happen unless there is injury in the arrangement of position.
And if the bones of the foot have a fracture and sometimes these bones are elevated between them, then place the patient's foot on the ground prepared in this way.
Approximate one foot on what is raised by those bones, approximate there, press, and guide it back into its place.
Approximate a plaster and stopper on top of it, and place a small table under the foot, with two handles that can be adjusted.
Approach the limb with a descending structure (such as a splint or bandage) around it, since you have wrapped it with cloth and for other reasons that are necessary.
After three or four days have passed, you must remove the bandage, because you will find that the bones are broken or dislocated, and the operation in those cases is thus necessary.
But if the bones are fractured, you must restore them and guide them back into place as I have shown you and as I recommend to you in the treatment of hands. Approximate the fractured finger with the length of the finger, and place it slightly oblique to it; and place under the foot this table which I have recommended to you, and hold it with a descending structure.
But if the fracture of the bones is two or three or more, you must place a splint on each finger according to its size wrapped in linen cloth, and hold the table under the heel of the foot, and stand on one of the handles of the table away from the belly of the foot to ensure good retention.
It is appropriate for you to remember that you must consider all the causes which I have mentioned to you in other fractures, and be prepared for any eventuality.
Capital XVIII.
On the fracture of the neck of the woman and the pelvis bone and the coccyx of the man.
When the neck of the woman is fractured, you must let it rest in a horizontal position, and incline it slightly to one side, and hold it behind.
And the midwife fills the neck of the bed with cotton and places it in this manner; and the woman is shaken, and gradually raises her legs a little, because everything will be forgotten in front of the neck until it is in the correct position and the fractured bone returns to its place.
And place some pillows under the legs, and when she wants to urinate, move the bed with ease, and empty it and return it to its original position as described in the beginning, and bring back the first pillow.
Do this for seven days or more, because it will heal.
If you wish, take an ovine bladder and place it over the crack with a reed cannula; insert the entire bladder into the neck of the bone, and inflate the cannula with air inside until the bladder is filled with the bone, because the fracture will return.
And let it be filled with cotton and remain for as many days as I have recommended, until it is healed.
And when the pelvis bone of a man or woman is fractured, you must administer for its restoration and treatment what I have recommended in the treatment of the hip bone.
What is correct should not be hidden in these close fractures and what little it may be, especially for the one who has some study and experience and understands my words; because that fracture signifies to you the greatest dispositions via righteousness in its restoration and in its treatment, and therefore understand well.
But in the coccyx of the man when it is fractured, take a walnut shell, and place the coccyx inside it; and place it on a cloth covering, wrap it, and let it rest for three days or more, until it is healed.
Capital XIX.
On the restoration of fractured bones when they are ulcerated.
It is appropriate for the one who suffers from a fracture with an ulcer, and especially if the bone is large such as the hip or humerus, or similar to it, that you cauterize and probe it immediately if the conditions of cauterization are present as described above.
If the ulcer exudes pus, you must ensure its retention until you apply powdered tertian antimony to it if another cause is not present.
And begin the restoration of the fracture on that day, and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
But if an abscess occurs, you must leave the restoration of it for nine days, until the abscess subsides, and do not approach it on the third and fourth day altogether, because you will cause harm.
But if the fractured bone is prominent on the skin surface, you must ensure that you perforate it and guide it back with your hands and minimal extension.
But if the extension is not prepared and the situation of it with your hands, you must reduce it with this tool; and the tool that should be made of iron, its length should be seven or eight inches, and its width according to the size of the ulcer; and the physician should take three or four of these depending on the amount required for treatment in all types of fractures.
And let it be round, and let it be slightly dirty so that it is not doubled over during the operation.
And let it have a pointed end with a margin at its edges; let the thickness of it be increasing towards the top and from the base to the parts.
And this is the shape of it:
And it is called "labre volentz atelarem" in Greek, which means a slightly curved spatula.
Therefore, it must be placed on top of the bone's protruding extremity and held stationary.
And press that area until the bone returns and is held with some pressure.
And therefore, apply pressure to the fracture's extremities, and if the broken extremity is thin, do not use the instrument well because of good reason, then you must bore into that bone until the instrument has the power to do so.
But if you cannot reduce the bone entirely with what we have recommended, then bore it with what is appropriate for the borers that we have mentioned; or it will be with one of the saws in any manner that is possible for you.
And smooth that which remains in the bone with roughness and small cuts.
And when the bone is reduced, and the patient feels intense pain returning, then you know that the bone has not returned to its natural location; therefore, do what is necessary for the patient's great benefit.
When your restoration of the bone is complete, and then immerse linen in black stiff vinegar, and if it's summer, do not put oil on the wound or any other cause where there might be oil, to prevent pus and rot from occurring.
And apply needles at the time of your completion of the bone's restoration, and leave the wound exposed so that you bore it with forceps and use bandages according to the size of the wound.
Be careful not to press the wound with the fracture, because many times foolish doctors have done such things, and it has caused death or severe illness in their patients, which is a great sin. And if your ligature is light, soft, different from the ligatures of other fractures.
If the wound is large or a bad wound, and you fear any of the bad accidents that we have recommended, and pain occurs in the area, then it is not necessary to press on the needles, but apply a hard bandage where the needles are and tighten it with those.
And when the wound begins after one or two days, which is a sign of putrefaction, you must remove the linen that you had placed over it with vinegar, and then apply warm compresses, anoint them with balms, as our custom is to treat wounds, such as tetra-pharmacon and similar ones.
It is necessary for you to remove the ligament and examine the wound every morning and evening until it is consolidated and closed, and it must be prepared in such a way that the pus runs from the site to the parts easily.
If the wound lasts for many days without being consolidated or abscessed from the putrefaction, then you know that here are the signs of gangrene, and therefore you must examine the wound with care. And from these signs, those that separate and are extracted, those that do not separate and make the member swell and cause pain, you must bore them as is possible for you.
If pus or corruption occurs in the wound, it is staphylococcus or another form of decay and putrefaction, then you must press on one of those with what is appropriate for the treatment of that disease as described in its chapter.
And from that, the most important thing and you should present your case: when a large bone is broken, elevate the limb in such a way as the bones of the thigh and hip and similar ones of the large members, and do not presume attraction or extraction because many times it leads to death; leave it inside for as long as possible, because strong leeches may fall from it on their own within 20 to 30 days. Treat the wound if it has been observed by you, and do not leave it unattended.
The Capitulum XX (Chapter 20) begins here:
On the treatment of knots that occur in the wound of any fracture.
Knots often occur in the wound of healing from a fracture, and particularly near joints, and are caused by the swelling of the limb; and it greatly hinders the movement of the limb in its natural operation.
Therefore consider whether the knot is recent and administer medicines that have astringent properties such as alum, and enses, and mira, and similar substances; take some or all of these, and bind them with linen, or albumen of egg, or vinegar, and place it on the knot in a compress, and bind it firmly with good binding.
Leave the extraction alone for several days; then loosen it and apply another one until the knot is gone; or strengthen on top of that a lead plate firmly, because lead has the property of keeping anything that is swollen in the limbs.
But if the knot has already become hard and tight and is causing a constant pain, cut it through the most superficial part, and remove the excess or pierce it with sharp scissors until it is gone; and heal that until it heals.
The Capitulum XXI (Chapter 21) begins here:
On the restoration of fractures when they have been restored and the limb is held in position according to its nature.
When a bone fracture is restored, and the limb is held in position, it should not be done except for several reasons: one of which is excessive use of solutions of lime and lack of proper binding; or excess tightness of the bindings before the nutriment is prohibited from running to the limb; or excessive swelling of the bandages; or superfluous movement not at its time; or scanty blood in the body of the patient and debility.
And treatment for this is to give the patient food and make his heart tender until the blood is multiplied in him; administer a bath, and apply oil and comfort on it.
And apply a plaster on the limb, so that the plaster attracts little nourishment, or continuous swelling of the limb with hot water, until the nourishment runs and returns to its normal shape.
The Capitulum XXII (Chapter 22) begins here:
On the treatment of broken bones that are restored incorrectly and prevent the operation of them as required.
When there is a problem with the limb that has been restored already in healing from curvature and prominence of the fractured bone and knots, it is because the shape of the limb is distorted, but nevertheless the limb is not prohibited from its natural operation, then it is not necessary for you to receive the word of the one who claims that the limb was newly fractured.
But there are many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land, and this operation is very blameworthy, causing great fear of the punishment of which death is the penalty.
But if the limpness and knots are recent, then it is necessary to swaddle them with hot water in which soft herbs that soothe are placed, such as althea leaves and radishes, melilot, and similar substances; and make a compress with mollifying plasters such as diallaquilon well made, or take mucilage from althea roots and mix it with gray of gauline or olive oil, and apply it, or prepare figs rich in oil and grated with the fat of pigeon, and similar substances of medicines that are called ameliorating consolidation.
Sometimes it is dissolved by persistent, gentle friction that is applied to the moistest areas, and movement of the member should be administered all around at all hours.
Sometimes it is necessary to cut through the swelling strongly and convert it into stone for treatment, then the pus above must be drained and the continuation of the bone should be dissolved, and whatever excess of hardness from that meat should be removed with fine cutters, and ease should be administered in that area with solicitude and care.
Capital. xxiii. (This is a notation indicating the twenty-third chapter of the text.)
On the discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is essentially one of the joints' locations where movement is restricted, and the figure of the member is distorted, causing pain and serious injuries in the patient.
Therefore, when there is a dislocation, it must be treated immediately by reduction, and the reduction should not be delayed, for if it is delayed, the location becomes necrotic and it becomes difficult to reduce the dislocation.
And for this reason it is not good nor advisable to perform the amputation during the necrotic phase, for many times it causes spasms and painful contractions.
But when necrosis occurs, it is necessary to perform the amputation on the patient, and let it rest before removing the necrotic tissue; then the member should be embalmed with warm water and oil, and reduced with ease.
And the entire member must be treated with a substance that will bring about healing at its location.
And it has been divided into chapters on dislocation as was done with fractures; from the upper to lower parts of the body.
Capital. xxiv. (This is a notation indicating the twenty-fourth chapter of the text.)
On the treatment of dislocation of the jaw, lower.
If both mandibles are dislocated, it should not be delayed; and a dislocation of both is made in one of two ways: either they are reduced back to their locations with little movement, and are softened somewhat; or they are completely dislocated and displaced, until they are softened at the juncture and the patient's saliva flows out and cannot be held nor can they close their mouth, and their tongue is swollen in its socket.
But if the dislocation is slight, then it can be reduced with fewer measures.
But if the dislocation is complete and severe, it must be reduced gently and without delay.
And the attendant should hold the patient's head, and enter their thumb into the affected side of the dislocated jaw within the mouth, if the dislocation is on one side; or insert both thumbs simultaneously, if the dislocation is on both sides, and the others should be outside to support it; and tell the patient to stop moving their jaws and hold them apart from each other; and the doctor will gently guide the jaw back into its location.
And if the reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are dislocated, administer embalming with warm water and oil until it becomes easier to reduce both, and do not delay the reduction of both, as was said before.
And when they are returned and closed, and the patient's mouth is not yet softened, place powdered cloth on them with wax and oil, and make a paste of wax and warm oil; and let it be applied gently and gradually.
And let the patient rest on their back, with their head between two pillows to prevent them from turning to the right or left; and do not force them to eat anything; allow them a leon-like diet until the pain subsides and the jaws are healed, then administer whatever they desire.
And this should be done gently, and avoid forcing the opening of the mouth due to eating, drinking, speaking, and chewing, until the jaw is healed.
And if the reduction of both jaws is difficult when they are dislocated at some time and not converted back to their locations, it often leads to fevers and constant sweating, and severe weakness occurs in the patient's abdomen, and violent, pure vomiting follows.
And when you see this, you should know that it is a disaster; and many times the patient dies within ten days of experiencing this.
Capital. xxv. (This is a notation indicating the twenty-fifth chapter of the text.)
On the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the muscle.
The collarbone is not dislocated from the base through its continuity with the ribs; and sometimes it is dislocated at the outer edge, and this can be confirmed by palpation.
The restoration of him/her (the patient) is that he/she walks on his/her own two feet and extends his/her arms. Then press the area with your palm using strong pressure, because it will return (the intestine or hernia).
Then place a cushion over that plaster and pound it firmly.
If the extremity that is cut off and joined together is not dislocated in any day, then it must be reduced and sutured as you have said and as you are equipped for.
Then place a cushion over that plaster, pound it, cut through the flesh, and order the patient to rest and remain still until he/she is healed.

You should know that a limb or body part (muscle) can be dislocated in three ways, of which one is that it is dislocated at the joint where it meets another bone. This implies that the text is discussing the various types and causes of dislocations, and possibly different methods for treating them depending on their location. It may also provide information about the symptoms, signs, and potential complications associated with each type of dislocation.
The second way is that it (the limb or body part) can be dislocated at the joint above the muscle, and this is often the case; it is not dislocated at the joints behind by the shoulder, and it is not dislocated at the joints in front by the nerves.
Furthermore, a limb or body part (muscle) can be dislocated and extend beyond the joint at the back, but this occurs more often in those with thin skin; however, it is difficult to extend beyond the joint in muscles with thicker skin.
A heavy blow or accident can occur in some men, causing an abscess (apostema) on the muscle of that person; and it must be drained with a cautery and treated once it is already dislocated.
Therefore, you must first assess whether you are equipped for the procedure and then proceed with the treatment of it (the injury or condition).
The dislocation at the back joint where it meets another bone is known; therefore, you should distinguish between a dislocated limb or body part and an intact one, because there are clear differences among them; and you must feel for the concavity at the head of the muscle (the end of the bone), and at the back joint of the bone, you must touch the head of the muscle in such a way that it feels like touching the heel of a shoe. Furthermore, the patient is unable to reduce his/her hand to his/her elbow, or to move it in any way.
If it is a dislocation of the foot or ankle, you should feel for clear indications and not miss them.
And with this type of dislocation, reduction is made easily when it is fresh and the patient is a child.
The reduction is that the servant raises the patient's arm above the armpits; and he presses the affected joint downwards, lifting the joint with force towards its own location, the servant raises the patient's arm, and extends it thus; and he presses it downwards so that it returns easily.
If it does not return as a result of what has been said, and is due to the beginning of dislocation, many days later, then the patient must be bathed in hot water and given an ointment that softens and soothes like if it were a burn from aloe, fenugreek, and marshmallow in water, and it must be administered.
The patient should be positioned on his back and his affected joint should be pressed downwards with moderate pressure, awaiting the arrival of wool that is between softness and tightness, and the doctor should press his heel on the sphere (joint), lifting the muscle with force, pulling the patient's arm away from the opposite side to prevent it from becoming swollen, and another servant should hold the patient's head so that it does not become swollen at the opposite side, as the joint will soon return to its own location.
And if you want to do this, reduce the joint in the following way: and it is that you bring a man who is longer than the patient, from the side of that part, intertwine his muscle with the affected joint and lift the affected joint towards the armpits until the patient is suspended in the air, and another servant pulls the patient's arm away from the opposite side of the abdomen. If the patient is lightweight, it is necessary that something else be suspended, to weigh it down, as the dislocation will soon return.
The joint is reduced in another way: and it is that it be placed on the ground, a long post with its head round, like a mortar pestle, neither too thick nor too thin, should be placed near the patient; then you place cloths over the head of the post, and the patient stands according to the length of the post, and extend the patient's arm towards the opposite side; and the other part is extended with force, as the joint returns quickly to its own location.
If the reduction of the joint is difficult due to all the causes mentioned, then use this cure: and it is that you take a post whose length is two forearms and its width four fingers, and its thickness should be reduced at the end of the pestle in the following way:
And place cloths over the round head of the post, to prevent the post from hurting the patient.
And place the affected joint near the post and extend the arm or shoulder over the post towards the opposite side, and tie the post around the arm, shoulder, and end of the hand.
Place the arm on a step of the stairs according to its width, and extend the hand towards the opposite side, and suspend the other body part from the opposite side, as the joint will soon be restored.
And when the instrument for reducing the joint is complete in any way that is possible to you, then you should press downwards on the affected joint with wool of a suitable size; and apply a plaster prepared with powder of molasses and egg white over the entire muscle, so that it is rolled up above, and tighten the affected joint with moderate pressure; and allow the linen to be rolled onto the plaster, and bind the arm to the neck, leaving it loose, and do not let the arm swell for seven days.
It is necessary that the food of the patient be sparse, until the member is comfortable, as it is easier for healing to occur.
And approach food should be given more slowly within seven or five days and exercise the affected member; and if it is firm and not softened, then it has healed.
If the joint is dislocated for more than seven days due to moisture or other cause, then it is necessary that a cauterization with three cuts be administered in it, as it was said earlier in the chapter on cauteries.
If all of the above is done and the binding and joint are not closed within seven days, and the plaster and tightening are removed from it after some days, and it remains loose, swollen, and cannot lift the arm towards the armpits above, then you should know that the nerves in it (the muscle) are injured or stretched or softened, and you should know that the joint will never be placed back in its own location.
The reduction of the dislocation on the feet and the heel or the buttocks is done by pushing and extending with force until it returns.
And apply another tightening to it, and care for it until it is healed.
And if gravity and slowness in the movement of the member occur during healing, then the patient should bathe it for many days until that gravity is alleviated and returns to its original nature.
Chapter 27.
Treatise on the treatment of shoulder dislocation
Know that the shoulder joint is difficult to treat and has a similar return; and when it is dislocated in all parts, particularly in front or behind, the dislocation of this part is not hidden, since the shoulder may be displaced by falling or hitting in any way, depending on its figure, and when you adjust the dislocated shoulder with the affected arm, that is made clear to you, for the joint is concave and cannot fold the arm, nor can it touch the corresponding muscle.
And it is necessary to catch the shoulder and reduce the dislocation immediately in front where there is an evident swelling, for if it comes to a visible swelling, the reduction of this becomes difficult, and complete healing is not easy; and especially when it is at the back part, because all other types of dislocation are worse than this one, and it causes more pain, and many times this condition leads to death.
The restoration of the shoulder, when possible, is that the arm of it should be extended with two hands, and the shoulder should be expanded, and the doctor's hands should be on the shoulder and below it, and he pushes the joint with the fingers of his hands towards its original place, or with a rod in the palm of his hand until it returns to its place.
But if the dislocation is at the front part, then return it by bending the arm with your hand, and striking the shoulder with the edge of your palm.
And if the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then apply strong extension and movement.
And two servants should extend the arm, while another two hold it in place to prevent it from going back during extension.
And turn the arm around in all directions, since it is wrapped with long cloth or bandages around its hand.
And when the doctor touches the joint with his hand, he should anoint it with some oil to facilitate lubrication of the joint; and then push the joint forcefully until it returns.
Before the reduction of this joint, it should be stopped on a poultice that has astringent and healing properties with egg white, and it should be pressed with a tight bandage, and the arm should be suspended from the neck, and it should be left for several days.
And then unwrap it; and if the joint is formed in its place, you should remove the bandage from there.
And if the joint is not strengthened, you should apply another poultice and bandage for several days until it is tightened; and then remove that.
But if there is a risk of complications in the reduction of the joint and its position is moving, you should apply humectation in bathing and gentle friction, and compression, until it is relieved; or place an oil-soaked cloth on the affected joint (something greasy); and tie it around the bone, leaving it for a day and night. Then remove the oil-soaked cloth and put it in a bath; when it dries, you should rub the joint with warm friction, and apply a warming poultice to it three times a day, the first time, the second time, and the third time. Corruption has occurred within the intestine, so it should be bound with a bandage until it is healed.
And if you wish, place a wet and warm cowhide poultice over the wound with plaster and pressure on it; do this several times, because it is moist and reduces it to its original position.
Chapter XXVIII.
Treatise on the treatment of dislocation of the arm-shoulder joint.
The shoulder joint is sometimes dislocated and the reduction of the dislocation is easy, but the opposite is true for other joints; however, speed in reducing it at the time of dislocation is essential before the location becomes swollen or there is a visible swelling.
The reduction of the dislocation is that you place the patient's shoulder on a table and have the servant extend the arm of the patient; then, the doctor places his hand or palm near the joint and pushes it forcefully until it returns to its original position.
However, it is essential to consider whether the palm has already been placed on the patient's abdomen; in this case, the patient should place his two hands on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is preceding (precedent), you should position the hand of the affected side intrinsically on the table, so that the doctor's hand can be placed on the edge of the joint.
And if it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, you should strengthen it with a poultice allowing the swelling to subside and leave it; do not rub it, because it may not bear or endure on the reduction of this joint for more than several days.
However, the joint remains crooked in its shape; and you should not inform the patient of any cause, but only soften the arm so that it cannot strengthen any cause; in this case, you should know that the nerves are broken or severed, and you should not tamper with it unless it is tightened with a hot cautery to prevent further damage.
When the patient has recovered, place a plaster on it that we have recommended, let it rest and strengthen for five days; then remove it, let it rest again and exercise the affected hand.
If the condition of the area worsens and there is any serious complication, then soften it with warm water and massage it for several days until it improves.
Chapter XXIX.
On the treatment of dislocation of the fingers.
Sometimes, the fingers are dislocated completely.
And when one of those fingers is dislocated at the base of the palm or the back of it, stretch the finger and push on the dislocation until it returns to its normal position.
Then support the knuckle of the finger and suspend the affected part from it for two days.
Then suspend it and extend it until it is properly aligned on that day.
And at night, follow this procedure, and do not remove the bandage during the day but exercise it with movement, and do so at night; continue this for several days until it becomes firm.
And do the same if it is dislocated at the back of the hand; align it with that part and perform the first treatment until it heals.
Perform the same procedure when it is dislocated in other places.
Chapter XXX.
On the treatment of dislocation of the spine (specifically the vertebrae).
When one of the vertebrae of the back or neck is completely dislocated and several vertebrae are moved from their places, then there is no treatment for it, because death is imminent for the patient.
The sign of this condition is that the patient's excretion is outside their control and they cannot hold it in; several members of the body or two legs or one arm, or a vertebra, are softened or dislocated in four places because several vertebrae are dislocated and several are moved.
Therefore, it has been dislocated in the rear parts and is called scoliosis.
However, the treatment of scoliosis depends on whether it occurs in childhood, as there is no treatment for it or cure in that case.
But in cases of scoliosis resulting from accident, injury, or similar causes, the ancient writers have described various treatment methods at length, the most effective of which will be discussed here; however, due to the brevity required for this presentation, I will summarize it and provide an explanation of the essential principles using instruments that will be explained and illustrated.
Only scoliosis occurring in the upper back can be treated with the method I recommend.
The patient should lie face down with the affected area near a wall and be extended slightly away from the wall to avoid putting pressure on the feet.
A straight stick should be placed under the head with its tip in the ground near the patient's feet, and another stick should be used to lift the feet on the other end of the plank.
The operator should hold the stick and it should not be fixed in a hole; another assistant should hold the other end as described above.
Wrap the head of the patient with a cloth and then pull on the string firmly; extend the end of the string to the stick that is under the patient's head, and hold it there; then tighten the other string around the patient's hips and shoulders, and hold the chest and feet; finally, adjust all the strings and hold them on the other stick.
The operator should then extend the string to the other stick and ensure that neither stick nor the strings attached to their respective locations are moved, but they should not be fixed in place as previously mentioned.
The doctor should place his hands on the affected vertebra with force until it returns to its normal position, or he should place a table under it and adjust his feet on the table until it returns to its normal position.
But if it does not return to its normal position in this manner, then take a table that is one and a half cubits long or wide (or both) and carve a hole in the wall near the patient's head into which you can insert the end of the table. Then place the mattress of the table on the spine, and the doctor places his feet on the other end, and press strongly until the spine is compressed and returns to its place.
If you want, turn the body that is turned towards your hand; and it is that you place your feet on the head of the patient at the end of his fists, whose length should be one arm's length, and place the head of him in the same way two fists away from your feet; and let the distance between each fist be a palm, and make a hole in each fist through which the body passes, and each one should be firmly fixed to the ground so that it is not bad.
Make a fist around the area where the turn is, and roll it between the two holes made in each fist.
At the end of him is a hole through which the fist is formed, whose length is one palm in diameter as it is rolled; and the other two fists are similar to that one.
Then pull the ligaments that you have tied around the patient's feet in the hole that is to be his head, and the ligaments that you have tied around his thighs in the hole that is to be his legs.
Then place each body part and have a servant turn it with his hand, and the doctor guides the spine as previously stated.
Once the spine has returned and the area is closed, it is necessary to apply a healing paste made of albumen of eggs and stopper; and place a wooden stick over the paste, whose width should be three fingers or slightly more than that, and the length should take up the area of the swelling, and above the division of the spine.
Then use the ligament we mentioned for nutrition until it is healed.
If there remains any danger in the place at the end of healing, it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and smooth by long-term administration of a table, which we have recommended; and a sheet of lead has already been applied in that place.
Sometimes danger can occur in the end of the spines of the back, and it is due to dislocation; and that bone is already suspected of being weak, so it is not advisable to make an opening for it with this medicine, as strong pressure can lead to death.
Chapter .xxxi.
On the treatment of a dislocated pelvis.
Know that the joint of the pelvis and the joint of the muscle do not dislocate except by true dislocation, and it does not happen to those joints as it happens to other small joints with a slight displacement and concavity.
The joint of the pelvis is dislocated in four ways, as it can be dislocated at the internal divisions, or at the external divisions, or at the front divisions, or at the back divisions.
Moreover, it is more commonly dislocated at the internal divisions and less so at the front or back divisions.
The sign of the dislocation at the internal divisions is that when you adjust the thigh of the patient with the healthy one, it is longer and the inner edge is more prominent than the healthy one, and the patient cannot bend his leg without twisting it, and the area where it bends is marked with a cauterized wound, so that the head of the thigh has already been reached here.
The sign of the one to which external dislocation happens is that the symptoms of it are contrary to this symptom.
The sign of the one to which front dislocation happens is that the thigh expands according to its form, but it does not bend without pain in the front, and if it is pressed, it cannot go forward towards the front; and the urine of him is retained and the wound of him is marked, and you should walk on tiptoe.
The sign of the one to which back dislocation happens is that the front does not expand or bend in front of the wound, and the thigh is shorter than the other, and the wound is softened, and the head of the thigh is to be found there.
However, the reduction of this type of dislocation is that you consider whether the dislocation is old and long-standing, in which case it has already healed with your application of reduction and remains in its position; in that case, this treatment is not complete; therefore, no opening should be made.
In the case of a recent dislocation, which is one of the four types of dislocations, then you must attempt to return the joint and extend it inwardly and outwardly at the joints inside and outside, moving it to the left and right. This will naturally return it without needing any other treatment.
If it does not return by this method, then you must prepare strong medication and extend the joint from the lower part, either with your hands or a lime that is tied onto the joint above the wound; another minister should extend his hand upwards in such a way that he places his hands below the tendons of the dislocated limb; and another minister should firmly pull the rope at the root of the affected area, and another minister should hold the end of the rope tight.
And the extension (of the joint) should be from the lower part to the ankle at the angle of the collarbone, or from the lower part at the back to the joint of the spine.
And the extension should be done in one go, while the patient is raised from the ground and remains hanging.
And this type of extension is a common method for all four types.
Therefore, if the dislocation returns by what has been said, it will be good; otherwise, you must resort to whatever method of treatment that is specific for each case.
In the reduction (maneuver) for a dislocation, when the dislocation is inward at the joints, the patient should be laid on their back; and cushions should be placed on both sides of the affected area, from the root of the joint to where the thigh meets the groin. Then, extend the ropes outwards beyond the angle of the collarbone to the same level as the opposite shoulder joint.
And another strong minister should grasp the arms of the patient, and firmly pull the large part of the affected area outwards in the opposite direction using violent extension, as this will return it to its original position.
And this method is simpler for treating the affected limb and reducing it compared to other methods.
If it is excused from treatment using this method for any reason, then you must bind both of the patient's feet together with a strong and light rope, running it through both ankles and both wrists.
And the length of each part (of the rope) should be four fingers, while the affected limb should be extended more than the other by two fingers' width.
And the patient should be suspended over the top of the post, and at a height; and the distance from the ground to the patient should be two arm-lengths.
And command a strong young man to strike the head of the affected area and suspend another strong young man from the patient. The third young man should pull on the affected limb with force to push it back into its original position, as it will return quickly in this manner.
And in the reduction (maneuver), when the dislocation is outward at the joints, have the patient lie on his back as we have previously instructed; and tighten the ropes around the affected area and the feet of the patient.
And two posts should be placed, one under the feet and the other at the head; and adjust two more posts to the middle of the beam that is fixed on top of which moistened cloths should be wrapped, so that the patient is not discomforted by the post being between their legs. This prevents the extension at the affected joints from being too tight.
And each minister should extend his part, and the doctor with his hand guides the reduction according to art.
When the dislocation is at the hips, then the leg should be gently extended, as described in this narrative; and the doctor places the palm of their right hand on the affected hip, holds it there with the left, and applies traction to the extended leg away from the hip on the opposite side.
If the dislocation is at the hips behind, then it is not necessary for the patient to be extended on their back and lifted off the ground; instead, a hard object should be placed under the affected hip, as described earlier if the groin area is dislocated outside the body (as mentioned earlier, placing two hard objects beneath them will help with the positioning). The patient should lie on their face, and their limbs should be extended according to what was previously mentioned, as there seems to be no benefit in doing so.
A compress should be applied with a board to the joint area where the dislocation occurred.
So, as the reduction process is complex (and its sign is not hidden from you), it's essential that you extend both of the patient's legs and observe when their leg muscles relax and their affected limb extends with no hesitation; at this point, you should know which member needs to be returned (based on the situation). Then, you adjust two cushions and place a compress and bandage in such a way that the intestine is not injured in any of the areas; hold the patient down for three or four days.
Then remove the bandage and compress, and compare one leg with the other.
If you observe that the muscles have relaxed, it means the dislocation has been corrected, and at that point, let the patient rest.
If you notice any signs of swelling or inflammation, then return and compress the affected area, extend it according to your initial instructions, and let it rest for three days.
Then remove the bandage and make the patient wait to walk for several days until they feel comfortable.
Chapter 32.
On treating hip dislocation.
The hip can be dislocated in three ways: it can be dislocated outside the body, inside the body, or more commonly behind, but not in front.
The sign of hip dislocation is when you instruct the patient to apply pressure on their hip, and if they cannot, it indicates that the hip is dislocated.
The restoration of all three dislocation methods is to position the patient correctly so that they can extend their leg if possible; place your hands on either side of the affected hip, and hold their foot between yours; press the affected hip with both palms, holding it firmly while another assistant extends the patient's leg until the hip returns to its original position.
The sign of hip reduction is when the patient's hip adheres to the bed without any effort.
Then compress the affected area and hold the hip to the bed, bind it with a bandage for three or four days, and then remove the bandages.
Do not allow the patient to walk for several days until they have recovered and feel comfortable.
If the reduction is not successful for you, administer strong traction on the muscles that were mentioned in the treatment of the thigh (preceding narrative).
Chapter 33.
On treating ankle dislocation.
The ankle is sometimes reduced by slight manipulation and other times requires compression, and the dislocation of the ankle can occur internally or externally.
The sign of ankle dislocation is that you observe swelling originating from the side where the dislocation occurred.
The treatment for ankle manipulation is gentle reduction and ensuring that it is easily extended and repositioned.
In the treatment of ankle dislocation caused by compression, it is necessary to position the patient correctly and have a strong and capable assistant from the opposite side of the affected limb in the best possible position.
Then you place your right hand on the top of the foot and your left hand on the lower part of the leg near the heel in the location of the cavity.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand, and with your left hand, grasp it twice and extend it while pushing the shin away from the cavity without strain. Then use your right arm to push the leg towards the hip joint.
And you shall do it twice as they have counted.
Then you push the legs of the foot onto the bed on the third observation, and you are at the head.
If it returns within an observation or two in this manner, and if the foot is swollen, that is good; and if not, then you shall repeat the operation, for he will return.
But if the reduction of it is forbidden by what they have recommended, then you lay the patient on his bed on the ground, and make a small hole in the ground, and place him inside, and you already have wrapped him with cloth so that you do not hurt the patient.
Then let the minister hold his legs, and another minister extend the foot with his hands or tied to the neck of the foot; and let each minister extend it to the extent of their reach, and the hole remain between the patient's legs, holding him so that the body is not stretched too far to the sides, as you must control its extension.
And the doctor shall bind the dislocation with his hands, and another minister holds the healthy limb to the sides, because the dislocation returns easily.
And therefore when the dislocation returns and is declared as your health of the reduction of it, then you pause plaster and place it firmly, and tie the foot to the bed.
And you must be careful or protect yourself from nerves which are behind the ankle, so that the ligament over it is not too tight and hurt him strongly.
Then leave it for two or three days; and if the ligament becomes softened, remove it.
Then loosen it on the third or fourth day, and forbid the patient from walking for forty days. But if he must walk in front of that space, it is not assured that the dislocation will not be dissolved and spoiled, and therefore it does not receive treatment.
And if it turns into an abscess cauterized, then you must provide for its expulsion in the place described in our account, in another place of care and binding, until it has healed.
Capital. XXXIV. (Capitulum Sanitatis Pedum: On the Health of the Feet)
On the cure of dislocation of the toes of the feet.
It must be bound with little extension without labor; and that which is not difficult, it will advance easily.
But if the dislocation is in one of the toes of the foot, then you must cause the patient to place his foot on a suitable surface such as the ground or a table, and be standing as if he were walking.
And you stand at the foot, and place your foot on something that is below the joints, and press it with your foot with force until it returns, and observe that it is bound and does not appear in a prominent position.
And you place the belly of the foot on a table that takes the entire foot, with two legs, and press it firmly and securely for three days; and then loosen it and make it rest from walking for several days until it is strengthened and the reduction is assured.
Capital. XXXV. (Capitulum Cauteria: On Cautery)
On the species of dislocation that are made by wound or fracture, or all together.
When any cause of these occurs, and you immerse yourself in the cure of that and in the restoration of it, many observations succeed death.
Therefore you must not presume over the cure of it, but one who is wise in the art, of long practice, compassionate and merciful, slow, neither reckless nor presumptuous; and that uses at the beginning of medicines that heal abscesses cauterized only, and leaves the patient to the divine judgment, but to him whom you hope for salvation from destruction, with alleviation of the illness, and in which any hope appears. And therefore you must do the reduction at the beginning of the cause before the abscess becomes cauterized.
And if the limb returns as we desire, then you administer the regimen that will relieve the abscess cauterized and heal the wound with what is appropriate for drying the wounds.
And if dislocation is with fracture and it occurs in separated joints, then you perform the treatment of them, and do as we said in simple illnesses before those places.
And guard and avoid as your virtue the instrument in the paths of fear according to what you have been preceded by my preaching; because that is better for you, and more permanent to your glory, and more healthy for your true bodies at your times. Thus ends the words of Albuccassis with which may God be blessed. Amen.
